<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Wind</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Natural movement of air or other gases relative to a planet's surface</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Wind_(disambiguation)" title="Wind (disambiguation)">Wind (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cherry_tree_moving_in_the_wind_1.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="533" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Cherry_tree_moving_in_the_wind_1.gif/300px-Cherry_tree_moving_in_the_wind_1.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Cherry_tree_moving_in_the_wind_1.gif/450px-Cherry_tree_moving_in_the_wind_1.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Cherry_tree_moving_in_the_wind_1.gif/600px-Cherry_tree_moving_in_the_wind_1.gif 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cherry_tree_moving_in_the_wind_1.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cherry tree moving with the wind blowing about 22 m/sec (about 79 km/h or 49 mph)</div></div></div>
<p><b>Wind</b> is the natural movement of <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth" title="Atmosphere of Earth">air</a> or other <a href="/wiki/Gas" title="Gas">gases</a> relative to a <a href="/wiki/Planetary_surface" title="Planetary surface">planet's surface</a>. Winds occur on a range of scales, from <a href="/wiki/Thunderstorm" title="Thunderstorm">thunderstorm</a> flows lasting tens of minutes, to local breezes generated by heating of <a href="/wiki/Land" title="Land">land</a> surfaces and lasting a few hours, to global winds resulting from the difference in <a href="/wiki/Absorption_(electromagnetic_radiation)" title="Absorption (electromagnetic radiation)">absorption</a> of <a href="/wiki/Solar_energy" title="Solar energy">solar energy</a> between the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Climate_zone" title="Climate zone">climate zones</a> on <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>. The two main causes of large-scale <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_circulation" title="Atmospheric circulation">atmospheric circulation</a> are the differential heating between the equator and the poles, and the rotation of the planet (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coriolis_effect" title="Coriolis effect">Coriolis effect</a>). Within the tropics and subtropics, <a href="/wiki/Thermal_low" title="Thermal low">thermal low</a> circulations over terrain and high plateaus can drive <a href="/wiki/Monsoon" title="Monsoon">monsoon</a> circulations. In coastal areas the <a href="/wiki/Sea_breeze" title="Sea breeze">sea breeze</a>/land breeze cycle can define local winds; in areas that have variable terrain, mountain and valley breezes can prevail.  
</p><p>Winds are commonly classified by their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scale_(spatial)" title="Scale (spatial)">spatial scale</a>, their <a href="/wiki/Speed" title="Speed">speed</a> and direction, the forces that cause them, the regions in which they occur, and their effect. Winds have various aspects: <a href="/wiki/Velocity" title="Velocity">velocity</a> (<a href="/wiki/Wind_speed" title="Wind speed">wind speed</a>); the density of the gas involved; energy content, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wind_energy" title="Wind energy">wind energy</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Meteorology" title="Meteorology">meteorology</a>, winds are often referred to according to their strength, and the direction from which the wind is blowing. The convention for directions refer to where the wind comes from; therefore, a 'western' or 'westerly' wind blows from the west to the east, a 'northern' wind blows south, and so on. This is sometimes counter-intuitive.
Short bursts of high speed wind are termed <a href="/wiki/Wind_gust" title="Wind gust">gusts</a>. Strong winds of intermediate duration (around one minute) are termed <a href="/wiki/Squall" title="Squall">squalls</a>. Long-duration winds have various names associated with their average strength, such as breeze, <a href="/wiki/Gale" title="Gale">gale</a>, <a href="/wiki/Storm#Classification" title="Storm">storm</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hurricane" title="Hurricane">hurricane</a>.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Outer_space" title="Outer space">outer space</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a> is the movement of gases or charged particles from the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> through space, while <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planetary_wind" title="Planetary wind">planetary wind</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Outgassing" title="Outgassing">outgassing</a> of light <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical elements</a> from a planet's atmosphere into space. The strongest observed winds on a planet in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> occur on <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>.
</p><p>In human civilization, the concept of wind has been explored in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mythology" title="Mythology">mythology</a>, influenced the events of history, expanded the range of transport and warfare, and provided a <a href="/wiki/Wind_power" title="Wind power">power source</a> for mechanical work, electricity, and recreation. Wind powers the voyages of <a href="/wiki/Sailing_ship" title="Sailing ship">sailing ships</a> across Earth's oceans. <a href="/wiki/Hot_air_balloon" title="Hot air balloon">Hot air balloons</a> use the wind to take short trips, and powered flight uses it to increase lift and reduce fuel consumption. Areas of <a href="/wiki/Wind_shear" title="Wind shear">wind shear</a> caused by various weather phenomena can lead to dangerous situations for aircraft. When winds become strong, trees and human-made structures can be damaged or destroyed.
</p><p>Winds can shape landforms, via a variety of <a href="/wiki/Aeolian_processes" title="Aeolian processes">aeolian processes</a> such as the formation of fertile soils, for example <a href="/wiki/Loess" title="Loess">loess</a>, and by <a href="/wiki/Erosion" title="Erosion">erosion</a>. Dust from large deserts can be moved great distances from its source region by the <a href="/wiki/Prevailing_winds" title="Prevailing winds">prevailing winds</a>; winds that are accelerated by rough topography and associated with dust outbreaks have been assigned regional names in various parts of the world because of their significant effects on those regions. Wind also affects the spread of wildfires. Winds can disperse seeds from various plants, enabling the survival and dispersal of those plant species, as well as flying insect and bird populations. When combined with cold temperatures, the wind has a negative impact on livestock. Wind affects animals' food stores, as well as their hunting and defensive strategies.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Causes">Causes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Causes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_pressure" title="Atmospheric pressure">Atmospheric pressure</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:10_PM_March_12_surface_analysis_of_Great_Blizzard_of_1888.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1267" data-file-width="915" decoding="async" height="305" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/10_PM_March_12_surface_analysis_of_Great_Blizzard_of_1888.png/220px-10_PM_March_12_surface_analysis_of_Great_Blizzard_of_1888.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/10_PM_March_12_surface_analysis_of_Great_Blizzard_of_1888.png/330px-10_PM_March_12_surface_analysis_of_Great_Blizzard_of_1888.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/10_PM_March_12_surface_analysis_of_Great_Blizzard_of_1888.png/440px-10_PM_March_12_surface_analysis_of_Great_Blizzard_of_1888.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:10_PM_March_12_surface_analysis_of_Great_Blizzard_of_1888.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Surface_weather_analysis" title="Surface weather analysis">Surface analysis</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Blizzard_of_1888" title="Great Blizzard of 1888">Great Blizzard of 1888</a>. Areas with greater isobaric packing indicate higher winds.</div></div></div>
<p>Wind is caused by differences in atmospheric pressure, which are mainly due to temperature difference. When a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pressure_gradient_force" title="Pressure gradient force">difference in atmospheric pressure</a> exists, air moves from the higher to the lower pressure area, resulting in winds of various speeds. On a rotating planet, air will also be deflected by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coriolis_effect" title="Coriolis effect">Coriolis effect</a>, except exactly on the equator. Globally, the two major driving factors of large-scale wind patterns (the <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_circulation" title="Atmospheric circulation">atmospheric circulation</a>) are the differential heating between the equator and the poles (difference in absorption of <a href="/wiki/Solar_energy" title="Solar energy">solar energy</a> leading to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buoyancy_force" title="Buoyancy force">buoyancy forces</a>) and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coriolis_effect" title="Coriolis effect">rotation of the planet</a>. Outside the tropics and aloft from frictional effects of the surface, the large-scale winds tend to approach <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geostrophic_balance" title="Geostrophic balance">geostrophic balance</a>. Near the Earth's surface, <a href="/wiki/Friction" title="Friction">friction</a> causes the wind to be slower than it would be otherwise. Surface friction also causes winds to blow more inward into low-pressure areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>Winds defined by an equilibrium of physical forces are used in the decomposition and analysis of wind profiles. They are useful for simplifying the atmospheric <a href="/wiki/Equations_of_motion" title="Equations of motion">equations of motion</a> and for making qualitative arguments about the horizontal and vertical distribution of horizontal winds. The <a href="/wiki/Geostrophic_wind" title="Geostrophic wind">geostrophic wind</a> component is the result of the balance between Coriolis force and pressure gradient force. It flows parallel to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isobar_(meteorology)" title="Isobar (meteorology)">isobars</a> and approximates the flow above the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atmospheric_boundary_layer" title="Atmospheric boundary layer">atmospheric boundary layer</a> in the midlatitudes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Thermal_wind" title="Thermal wind">thermal wind</a> is the <i>difference</i> in the geostrophic wind between two levels in the atmosphere. It exists only in an atmosphere with horizontal <a href="/wiki/Temperature_gradient" title="Temperature gradient">temperature gradients</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Ageostrophy" title="Ageostrophy">ageostrophic wind</a> component is the difference between actual and geostrophic wind, which is responsible for air "filling up" cyclones over time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gradient_wind" title="Gradient wind">gradient wind</a> is similar to the geostrophic wind but also includes <a href="/wiki/Centrifugal_force" title="Centrifugal force">centrifugal force</a> (or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centripetal_acceleration" title="Centripetal acceleration">centripetal acceleration</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Measurement">Measurement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Measurement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Anemometer_2745.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2711" data-file-width="4179" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Anemometer_2745.JPG/220px-Anemometer_2745.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Anemometer_2745.JPG/330px-Anemometer_2745.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Anemometer_2745.JPG/440px-Anemometer_2745.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Anemometer_2745.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cup-type anemometer with vertical axis, a sensor on a remote meteorological station</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Occluded_mesocyclone_tornado5_-_NOAA.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1816" decoding="async" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Occluded_mesocyclone_tornado5_-_NOAA.jpg/220px-Occluded_mesocyclone_tornado5_-_NOAA.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Occluded_mesocyclone_tornado5_-_NOAA.jpg/330px-Occluded_mesocyclone_tornado5_-_NOAA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Occluded_mesocyclone_tornado5_-_NOAA.jpg/440px-Occluded_mesocyclone_tornado5_-_NOAA.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Occluded_mesocyclone_tornado5_-_NOAA.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An occluded mesocyclone tornado (Oklahoma, May 1999)</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Wind_direction" title="Wind direction">Wind direction</a> is usually expressed in terms of the direction from which it originates. For example, a <i>northerly</i> wind blows from the north to the south.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HOWTOREAD_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-HOWTOREAD-7">[7]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Weather_vane" title="Weather vane">Weather vanes</a> pivot to indicate the direction of the wind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> At airports, <a href="/wiki/Windsock" title="Windsock">windsocks</a> indicate wind direction, and can also be used to estimate wind speed by the angle of hang.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> Wind speed is measured by <a href="/wiki/Anemometer" title="Anemometer">anemometers</a>, most commonly using rotating cups or propellers. When a high measurement frequency is needed (such as in research applications), wind can be measured by the propagation speed of <a href="/wiki/Ultrasound" title="Ultrasound">ultrasound</a> signals or by the effect of ventilation on the resistance of a heated wire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Another type of anemometer uses <a href="/wiki/Pitot_tube" title="Pitot tube">pitot tubes</a> that take advantage of the pressure differential between an inner tube and an outer tube that is exposed to the wind to determine the dynamic pressure, which is then used to compute the wind speed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sustained wind speeds are reported globally at a 10 meters (33 ft) height and are averaged over a 10‑minute time frame. The United States reports winds over a 1‑minute average for tropical cyclones,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NWSM_Defs_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-NWSM_Defs-12">[12]</a></sup> and a 2‑minute average within weather observations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fmh1_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-fmh1-13">[13]</a></sup> India typically reports winds over a 3‑minute average.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Knowing the wind sampling average is important, as the value of a one-minute sustained wind is typically 14% greater than a ten-minute sustained wind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> A short burst of high speed wind is termed a <a href="/wiki/Wind_gust" title="Wind gust">wind gust</a>, one technical definition of a wind gust is: the maxima that exceed the lowest wind speed measured during a ten-minute time interval by 10 knots (5 m/s) for periods of seconds. A <a href="/wiki/Squall" title="Squall">squall</a> is an increase of the wind speed above a certain threshold, which lasts for a minute or more.
</p><p>To determine winds aloft, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rawinsondes" title="Rawinsondes">rawinsondes</a> determine wind speed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GPS" title="GPS">GPS</a>, <a href="/wiki/LORAN" title="LORAN">radio navigation</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Radar" title="Radar">radar</a> tracking of the probe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Alternatively, movement of the parent <a href="/wiki/Weather_balloon" title="Weather balloon">weather balloon</a> position can be tracked from the ground visually using <a href="/wiki/Theodolite" title="Theodolite">theodolites</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Remote_sensing" title="Remote sensing">Remote sensing</a> techniques for wind include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SODAR" title="SODAR">SODAR</a>, <a href="/wiki/Doppler_effect" title="Doppler effect">Doppler</a> <a href="/wiki/Lidar" title="Lidar">lidars</a> and radars, which can measure the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doppler_shift" title="Doppler shift">Doppler shift</a> of <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">electromagnetic radiation</a> scattered or reflected off suspended <a href="/wiki/Aerosol" title="Aerosol">aerosols</a> or <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecules</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Radiometer" title="Radiometer">radiometers</a> and radars can be used to measure the surface roughness of the ocean from space or airplanes. Ocean roughness can be used to estimate wind velocity close to the sea surface over oceans. Geostationary satellite imagery can be used to estimate the winds at cloud top based upon how far clouds move from one image to the next. <a href="/wiki/Wind_engineering" title="Wind engineering">Wind engineering</a> describes the study of the effects of the wind on the built environment, including buildings, bridges and other artificial objects.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Wind_force_scale">Wind force scale</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Wind force scale">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone_scales" title="Tropical cyclone scales">Tropical cyclone scales</a> and <a href="/wiki/Surface_weather_analysis" title="Surface weather analysis">Surface weather analysis</a></div><p>Historically, the <a href="/wiki/Beaufort_scale" title="Beaufort scale">Beaufort wind force scale</a> (created by <a href="/wiki/Francis_Beaufort" title="Francis Beaufort">Beaufort</a>) provides an empirical description of wind speed based on observed sea conditions. Originally it was a 13-level scale (0-12), but during the 1940s, the scale was expanded to 18 levels (0-17).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beaufort_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beaufort-18">[18]</a></sup> There are general terms that differentiate winds of different average speeds such as a breeze, a gale, a storm, or a hurricane. Within the Beaufort scale, gale-force winds lie between 28 knots (52 km/h) and 55 knots (102 km/h) with preceding adjectives such as moderate, fresh, strong, and whole used to differentiate the wind's strength within the gale category.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> A storm has winds of 56 knots (104 km/h) to 63 knots (117 km/h).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> The terminology for tropical cyclones differs from one region to another globally. Most ocean basins use the average wind speed to determine the tropical cyclone's category. Below is a summary of the classifications used by <a href="/wiki/Regional_Specialized_Meteorological_Centre" title="Regional Specialized Meteorological Centre">Regional Specialized Meteorological Centers</a> worldwide:
</p><table border="1" class="collapsible wikitable">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Enhanced_Fujita_scale">Enhanced Fujita scale</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Enhanced Fujita scale">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enhanced_Fujita_Scale" title="Enhanced Fujita Scale">Enhanced Fujita Scale</a> (EF Scale) rates the strength of tornadoes by using damage to estimate wind speed. It has six levels, from visible damage to complete destruction. It is used in the United States and some other countries with small modifications (among which include Canada and France).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Station_model">Station model</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Station model">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wind_barbs.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="588" data-file-width="464" decoding="async" height="279" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Wind_barbs.gif/220px-Wind_barbs.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Wind_barbs.gif/330px-Wind_barbs.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Wind_barbs.gif/440px-Wind_barbs.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wind_barbs.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Wind plotting within a station model</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Station_model" title="Station model">station model</a> plotted on surface <a href="/wiki/Weather_map" title="Weather map">weather maps</a> uses a wind barb to show both wind direction and speed. The wind barb shows the speed using "flags" on the end.
</p>
<ul><li>Each half of a flag depicts 5 knots (9.3 km/h) of wind.</li>
<li>Each full flag depicts 10 knots (19 km/h) of wind.</li>
<li>Each <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pennant_(commissioning)" title="Pennant (commissioning)">pennant</a> (filled triangle) depicts 50 knots (93 km/h) of wind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Winds are depicted as blowing from the direction the barb is facing. Therefore, a northeast wind will be depicted with a line extending from the cloud circle to the northeast, with flags indicating wind speed on the northeast end of this line.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-24">[24]</a></sup> Once plotted on a map, an analysis of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isotach" title="Isotach">isotachs</a> (lines of equal wind speeds) can be accomplished. Isotachs are particularly useful in diagnosing the location of the <a href="/wiki/Jet_stream" title="Jet stream">jet stream</a> on upper-level constant pressure charts, and are usually located at or above the 300 hPa level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Global_climatology">Global climatology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Global climatology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Prevailing_winds" title="Prevailing winds">Prevailing winds</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_prevailing_winds_on_earth.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="625" data-file-width="1425" decoding="async" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Map_prevailing_winds_on_earth.png/330px-Map_prevailing_winds_on_earth.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Map_prevailing_winds_on_earth.png/495px-Map_prevailing_winds_on_earth.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Map_prevailing_winds_on_earth.png/660px-Map_prevailing_winds_on_earth.png 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_prevailing_winds_on_earth.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The westerlies and trade winds</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Earth_Global_Circulation.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="477" data-file-width="556" decoding="async" height="283" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Earth_Global_Circulation.jpg/330px-Earth_Global_Circulation.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Earth_Global_Circulation.jpg/495px-Earth_Global_Circulation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6d/Earth_Global_Circulation.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Earth_Global_Circulation.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Winds are part of Earth's atmospheric circulation</div></div></div>
<p>Easterly winds, on average, dominate the flow pattern across the poles, westerly winds blow across the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mid-latitudes" title="Mid-latitudes">mid-latitudes</a> of the earth, polewards of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subtropical_ridge" title="Subtropical ridge">subtropical ridge</a>, while easterlies again dominate the <a href="/wiki/Tropics" title="Tropics">tropics</a>.
</p><p>Directly under the subtropical ridge are the doldrums, or horse latitudes, where winds are lighter. Many of the Earth's deserts lie near the average latitude of the subtropical ridge, where descent reduces the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Relative_humidity" title="Relative humidity">relative humidity</a> of the air mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> The strongest winds are in the mid-latitudes where cold polar air meets warm air from the tropics.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tropics">Tropics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Tropics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trade_wind" title="Trade wind">Trade wind</a> and <a href="/wiki/Monsoon" title="Monsoon">Monsoon</a></div>
<p>The trade winds (also called trades) are the prevailing pattern of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Easterlies" title="Easterlies">easterly</a> surface winds found in the tropics towards the Earth's <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equator</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> The trade winds blow predominantly from the northeast in the Northern Hemisphere and from the southeast in the Southern Hemisphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ralph_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ralph-28">[28]</a></sup> The trade winds act as the <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone#Steering_winds" title="Tropical cyclone">steering flow</a> for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclones" title="Tropical cyclones">tropical cyclones</a> that form over the world's oceans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Trade winds also steer African dust westward across the Atlantic Ocean into the Caribbean, as well as portions of southeast North America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pooraq_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-pooraq-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Monsoon" title="Monsoon">monsoon</a> is a seasonal prevailing wind that lasts for several months within tropical regions. The term was first used in English in India, <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>, Pakistan, and neighboring countries to refer to the big seasonal winds blowing from the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Sea" title="Arabian Sea">Arabian Sea</a> in the southwest bringing heavy rainfall to the area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Its poleward progression is accelerated by the development of a heat low over the Asian, African, and North American continents during May through July, and over Australia in December.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCFMRF_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-NCFMRF-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Westerlies_and_their_impact">Westerlies and their impact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Westerlies and their impact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Franklingulfstream.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2515" data-file-width="3089" decoding="async" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Franklingulfstream.jpg/220px-Franklingulfstream.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Franklingulfstream.jpg/330px-Franklingulfstream.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Franklingulfstream.jpg/440px-Franklingulfstream.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Franklingulfstream.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin" title="Benjamin Franklin">Benjamin Franklin</a>'s map of the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Stream" title="Gulf Stream">Gulf Stream</a></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Westerlies" title="Westerlies">Westerlies</a></div>
<p>The Westerlies or the Prevailing Westerlies are the <a href="/wiki/Prevailing_winds" title="Prevailing winds">prevailing winds</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_latitudes" title="Middle latitudes">middle latitudes</a> between 35 and 65 degrees <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitude</a>. These prevailing winds blow from the west to the east,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> and steer extratropical cyclones in this general manner. The winds are predominantly from the southwest in the Northern Hemisphere and from the northwest in the Southern Hemisphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ralph_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ralph-28">[28]</a></sup> They are strongest in the winter when the pressure is lower over the poles, and weakest during the summer and when pressures are higher over the poles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>Together with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trade_wind" title="Trade wind">trade winds</a>, the westerlies enabled a round-trip trade route for sailing ships crossing the Atlantic and Pacific Oceans, as the westerlies lead to the development of strong ocean currents on the western sides of oceans in both hemispheres through the process of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_intensification" title="Western intensification">western intensification</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> These western ocean currents transport warm, sub-tropical water polewards toward the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polar_region" title="Polar region">polar regions</a>. The westerlies can be particularly strong, especially in the southern hemisphere, where there is less land in the middle latitudes to cause the flow pattern to amplify, which slows the winds down. The strongest westerly winds in the middle latitudes are within a band known as the <a href="/wiki/Roaring_Forties" title="Roaring Forties">Roaring Forties</a>, between <a href="/wiki/40th_parallel_south" title="40th parallel south">40</a> and <a href="/wiki/50th_parallel_south" title="50th parallel south">50 degrees latitude south</a> of the equator.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> The Westerlies play an important role in carrying the warm, equatorial waters and winds to the western coasts of continents,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DRIFT_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-DRIFT-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> especially in the southern hemisphere because of its vast oceanic expanse.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Polar_easterlies">Polar easterlies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Polar easterlies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Polar_easterlies" title="Polar easterlies">Polar easterlies</a></div>
<p>The polar easterlies, also known as Polar Hadley cells, are dry, cold prevailing winds that blow from the high-pressure areas of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polar_high" title="Polar high">polar highs</a> at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_pole" title="North pole">north</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_pole" title="South pole">south poles</a> towards the low-pressure areas within the Westerlies at high latitudes. Unlike the Westerlies, these prevailing winds blow from the east to the west, and are often weak and irregular.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Because of the low sun angle, cold air builds up and <a href="/wiki/Subsidence_(atmosphere)" title="Subsidence (atmosphere)">subsides</a> at the pole creating surface high-pressure areas, forcing an equatorward outflow of air;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> that outflow is deflected westward by the Coriolis effect.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Local_considerations">Local considerations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Local considerations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:392px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_local_winds.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="625" data-file-width="1425" decoding="async" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Map_local_winds.png/390px-Map_local_winds.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Map_local_winds.png/585px-Map_local_winds.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Map_local_winds.png/780px-Map_local_winds.png 2x" width="390"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_local_winds.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Local winds around the world. These winds are formed through the heating of land (from mountains or flat terrain)</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sea_and_land_breezes">Sea and land breezes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Sea and land breezes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sea_breeze" title="Sea breeze">Sea breeze</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Diagrama_de_formacion_de_la_brisa-breeze.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="755" data-file-width="689" decoding="async" height="241" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Diagrama_de_formacion_de_la_brisa-breeze.svg/220px-Diagrama_de_formacion_de_la_brisa-breeze.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Diagrama_de_formacion_de_la_brisa-breeze.svg/330px-Diagrama_de_formacion_de_la_brisa-breeze.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Diagrama_de_formacion_de_la_brisa-breeze.svg/440px-Diagrama_de_formacion_de_la_brisa-breeze.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Diagrama_de_formacion_de_la_brisa-breeze.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A: Sea breeze (occurs at daytime), B: Land breeze (occurs at nighttime)</div></div></div>
<p>In coastal regions, sea breezes and land breezes can be important factors in a location's prevailing winds. The sea is warmed by the sun more slowly because of water's greater <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Specific_heat" title="Specific heat">specific heat</a> compared to land. As the temperature of the surface of the land rises, the land heats the air above it by conduction. The warm air is less dense than the surrounding environment and so it rises.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> The cooler air above the sea, now with higher <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_level_pressure" title="Sea level pressure">sea level pressure</a>, flows inland into the lower pressure, creating a cooler breeze near the coast. A background along-shore wind either strengthens or weakens the sea breeze, depending on its orientation with respect to the Coriolis force.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>At night, the land cools off more quickly than the ocean because of differences in their specific heat values. This temperature change causes the daytime sea breeze to dissipate. When the temperature onshore cools below the temperature offshore, the pressure over the water will be lower than that of the land, establishing a land breeze, as long as an onshore wind is not strong enough to oppose it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jet_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jet-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Near_mountains">Near mountains</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Near mountains">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vol_d%27onde.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="270" data-file-width="369" decoding="async" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Vol_d%27onde.svg/220px-Vol_d%27onde.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Vol_d%27onde.svg/330px-Vol_d%27onde.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Vol_d%27onde.svg/440px-Vol_d%27onde.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vol_d%27onde.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mountain wave schematic. The wind flows towards a mountain and produces a first oscillation (A). A second wave occurs further away and higher. The lenticular clouds form at the peak of the waves (B).</div></div></div>
<p>Over elevated surfaces, heating of the ground exceeds the heating of the surrounding air at the same altitude above <a href="/wiki/Sea_level" title="Sea level">sea level</a>, creating an associated thermal low over the terrain and enhancing any thermal lows that would have otherwise existed,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> and changing the wind circulation of the region. In areas where there is rugged <a href="/wiki/Topography" title="Topography">topography</a> that significantly interrupts the environmental wind flow, the wind circulation between mountains and valleys is the most important contributor to the prevailing winds. Hills and valleys substantially distort the airflow by increasing friction between the atmosphere and landmass by acting as a physical block to the flow, deflecting the wind parallel to the range just upstream of the topography, which is known as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Barrier_jet" title="Barrier jet">barrier jet</a>. This barrier jet can increase the low-level wind by 45%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Wind direction also changes because of the contour of the land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trex_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Trex-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>If there is a <a href="/wiki/Mountain_pass" title="Mountain pass">pass</a> in the mountain range, winds will rush through the pass with considerable speed because of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bernoulli_principle" title="Bernoulli principle">Bernoulli principle</a> that describes an inverse relationship between speed and pressure. The airflow can remain turbulent and erratic for some distance downwind into the flatter countryside. These conditions are dangerous to ascending and descending <a href="/wiki/Airplane" title="Airplane">airplanes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trex_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-Trex-50">[50]</a></sup> Cool winds accelerating through mountain gaps have been given regional names. In Central America, examples include the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Papagayo_wind" title="Papagayo wind">Papagayo wind</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Panama" title="Panama">Panama</a> wind, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tehuano_wind" title="Tehuano wind">Tehuano wind</a>. In Europe, similar winds are known as the <a href="/wiki/Bora_(wind)" title="Bora (wind)">Bora</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tramontane" title="Tramontane">Tramontane</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mistral_(wind)" title="Mistral (wind)">Mistral</a>. When these winds blow over open waters, they increase mixing of the upper layers of the ocean that elevates cool, nutrient rich waters to the surface, which leads to increased marine life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Papa_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Papa-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>In mountainous areas, local distortion of the airflow becomes severe. Jagged terrain combines to produce unpredictable flow patterns and turbulence, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lee_waves" title="Lee waves">rotors</a>, which can be topped by <a href="/wiki/Lenticular_cloud" title="Lenticular cloud">lenticular clouds</a>. Strong <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Updraft" title="Updraft">updrafts</a>, downdrafts, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eddies" title="Eddies">eddies</a> develop as the air flows over hills and down valleys. Orographic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Precipitation_(meteorology)" title="Precipitation (meteorology)">precipitation</a> occurs on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Windward" title="Windward">windward</a> side of mountains and is caused by the rising air motion of a large-scale flow of moist air across the mountain ridge, also known as upslope flow, resulting in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adiabatic_lapse_rate" title="Adiabatic lapse rate">adiabatic</a> cooling and condensation. In mountainous parts of the world subjected to relatively consistent winds (for example, the trade winds), a more moist climate usually prevails on the windward side of a mountain than on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leeward" title="Leeward">leeward</a> or downwind side. Moisture is removed by orographic lift, leaving drier air on the descending and generally warming, leeward side where a <a href="/wiki/Rain_shadow" title="Rain shadow">rain shadow</a> is observed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MT_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-MT-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>Winds that flow over mountains down into lower elevations are known as downslope winds. These winds are warm and dry. In Europe downwind of the <a href="/wiki/Alps" title="Alps">Alps</a>, they are known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Foehn" title="Foehn">foehn</a>. In Poland, an example is the <a href="/wiki/Halny" title="Halny">halny</a> wiatr. In Argentina, the local name for down sloped winds is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zonda_(wind)" title="Zonda (wind)">zonda</a>. In Java, the local name for such winds is koembang. In New Zealand, they are known as the <a href="/wiki/Nor%27west_arch" title="Nor'west arch">Nor'west arch</a>, and are accompanied by the cloud formation they are named after that has inspired artwork over the years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> In the Great Plains of the United States, these winds are known as a <a href="/wiki/Chinook_wind" title="Chinook wind">chinook</a>. Downslope winds also occur in the foothills of the Appalachian mountains of the United States,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> and they can be as strong as other downslope winds<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> and unusual compared to other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Foehn_winds" title="Foehn winds">foehn winds</a> in that the relative humidity typically changes little due to the increased moisture in the source air mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> In California, downslope winds are funneled through mountain passes, which intensify their effect, and examples include the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Santa_Ana_wind" title="Santa Ana wind">Santa Ana</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sundowner_(wind)" title="Sundowner (wind)">sundowner</a> winds. Wind speeds during downslope wind effect can exceed 160 kilometers per hour (99 mph).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-boulder_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-boulder-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Shear">Shear</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Shear">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hodographe_NOAA.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="648" data-file-width="553" decoding="async" height="328" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Hodographe_NOAA.PNG/280px-Hodographe_NOAA.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Hodographe_NOAA.PNG/420px-Hodographe_NOAA.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/21/Hodographe_NOAA.PNG 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hodographe_NOAA.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Hodograph" title="Hodograph">Hodograph</a> plot of wind vectors at various heights in the <a href="/wiki/Troposphere" title="Troposphere">troposphere</a>, which is used to diagnose vertical <a href="/wiki/Wind_shear" title="Wind shear">wind shear</a></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wind_shear" title="Wind shear">Wind shear</a></div>
<p>Wind shear, sometimes referred to as <a href="/wiki/Wind_gradient" title="Wind gradient">wind gradient</a>, is a difference in wind speed and direction over a relatively short distance in the Earth's atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Wind shear can be broken down into vertical and horizontal components, with horizontal wind shear seen across <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weather_fronts" title="Weather fronts">weather fronts</a> and near the coast,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DR_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-DR-59">[59]</a></sup> and vertical shear typically near the surface,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> though also at higher levels in the atmosphere near upper level jets and frontal zones aloft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>Wind shear itself is a <a href="/wiki/Microscale_meteorology" title="Microscale meteorology">microscale meteorological</a> phenomenon occurring over a very small distance, but it can be associated with <a href="/wiki/Mesoscale_meteorology" title="Mesoscale meteorology">mesoscale</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synoptic_scale" title="Synoptic scale">synoptic scale</a> weather features such as <a href="/wiki/Squall_line" title="Squall line">squall lines</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cold_front" title="Cold front">cold fronts</a>. It is commonly observed near <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microburst" title="Microburst">microbursts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Downburst" title="Downburst">downbursts</a> caused by <a href="/wiki/Thunderstorm" title="Thunderstorm">thunderstorms</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cleghorn_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cleghorn-62">[62]</a></sup> weather fronts, areas of locally higher low level winds referred to as low level jets, near mountains,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> radiation inversions that occur because of clear skies and calm winds, buildings,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Wind_turbine" title="Wind turbine">wind turbines</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Sailboat" title="Sailboat">sailboats</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Wind shear has a significant effect on the control of aircraft during take-off and landing,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> and was a significant cause of aircraft accidents involving large loss of life within the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cleghorn_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cleghorn-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sound movement through the atmosphere is affected by wind shear, which can bend the wave front, causing sounds to be heard where they normally would not, or vice versa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> Strong vertical wind shear within the troposphere also inhibits <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone" title="Tropical cyclone">tropical cyclone</a> development,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> but helps to organize individual thunderstorms into living longer life cycles that can then produce <a href="/wiki/Severe_weather" title="Severe weather">severe weather</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Thermal_wind" title="Thermal wind">thermal wind</a> concept explains how differences in wind speed with height are dependent on horizontal temperature differences, and explains the existence of the <a href="/wiki/Jet_stream" title="Jet stream">jet stream</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IP_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-IP-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_civilization">In civilization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: In civilization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As a natural force, the wind was often personified as one or more <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wind_god" title="Wind god">wind gods</a> or as an expression of the <a href="/wiki/Supernatural" title="Supernatural">supernatural</a> in many cultures. <a href="/wiki/Vayu" title="Vayu">Vayu</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Historical_Vedic_religion" title="Historical Vedic religion">Vedic</a> and Hindu God of Wind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jordan_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jordan-73">[73]</a></sup> The Greek wind gods include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boreas_(god)" title="Boreas (god)">Boreas</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_Goddess" title="Greek Goddess">Notus</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eurus" title="Eurus">Eurus</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zephyrus" title="Zephyrus">Zephyrus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jordan_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jordan-73">[73]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Aeolus" title="Aeolus">Aeolus</a>, in varying interpretations the ruler or keeper of the four winds, has also been described as <a href="/wiki/Astraeus" title="Astraeus">Astraeus</a>, the god of dusk who fathered the four winds with <a href="/wiki/Eos" title="Eos">Eos</a>, goddess of dawn. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greeks" title="Ancient Greeks">ancient Greeks</a> also observed the seasonal change of the winds, as evidenced by the <a href="/wiki/Tower_of_the_Winds" title="Tower of the Winds">Tower of the Winds</a> in <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jordan_73-2"><a href="#cite_note-Jordan-73">[73]</a></sup> Venti are the Roman gods of the winds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/F%C5%ABjin" title="Fūjin">Fūjin</a> is the Japanese wind god and is one of the eldest <a href="/wiki/Shinto" title="Shinto">Shinto</a> gods. According to legend, he was present at the creation of the world and first let the winds out of his bag to clear the world of mist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Norse_mythology" title="Norse mythology">Norse mythology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nj%C3%B6r%C3%B0r" title="Njörðr">Njörðr</a> is the god of the wind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jordan_73-3"><a href="#cite_note-Jordan-73">[73]</a></sup> There are also four dvärgar (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norse_dwarves" title="Norse dwarves">Norse dwarves</a>), named <a href="/wiki/Nor%C3%B0ri,_Su%C3%B0ri,_Austri_and_Vestri" title="Norðri, Suðri, Austri and Vestri">Norðri, Suðri, Austri and Vestri</a>, and probably the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Four_stags_of_Yggdrasil" title="Four stags of Yggdrasil">four stags of Yggdrasil</a>, personify the four winds, and parallel the four Greek wind gods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Stribog" title="Stribog">Stribog</a> is the name of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slavic_Mythology" title="Slavic Mythology">Slavic god</a> of winds, sky and air. He is said to be the ancestor (grandfather) of the winds of the eight directions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jordan_73-4"><a href="#cite_note-Jordan-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Kamikaze_(typhoon)" title="Kamikaze (typhoon)">Kamikaze</a> is a Japanese word, usually translated as divine wind, believed to be a gift from the gods. The term is first known to have been used as the name of a pair or series of typhoons that are said to have saved Japan from two Mongol fleets under Kublai Khan that attacked Japan in 1274 and again in 1281.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Wind" title="Protestant Wind">Protestant Wind</a> is a name for the storm that deterred the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Armada" title="Spanish Armada">Spanish Armada</a> from an invasion of England in 1588 where the wind played a pivotal role,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> or the favorable winds that enabled <a href="/wiki/William_III_of_England" title="William III of England">William of Orange</a> to invade England in 1688.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> During <a href="/wiki/Napoleon" title="Napoleon">Napoleon</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_Invasion_of_Egypt_(1798)" title="French Invasion of Egypt (1798)">Egyptian Campaign</a>, the French soldiers had a hard time with the <a href="/wiki/Khamsin" title="Khamsin">khamsin</a> wind: when the storm appeared "as a blood-stint in the distant sky", the Ottomans went to take cover, while the French "did not react until it was too late, then choked and fainted in the blinding, suffocating walls of dust".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> During the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_African_Campaign" title="North African Campaign">North African Campaign</a> of the World War II, "allied and German troops were several times forced to halt in mid-battle because of sandstorms caused by khamsin... Grains of sand whirled by the wind blinded the soldiers and created electrical disturbances that rendered compasses useless."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transportation">Transportation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Transportation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Exeter-20may44.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="555" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="204" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Exeter-20may44.jpg/220px-Exeter-20may44.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Exeter-20may44.jpg/330px-Exeter-20may44.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Exeter-20may44.jpg/440px-Exeter-20may44.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Exeter-20may44.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exeter_International_Airport" title="Exeter International Airport">RAF Exeter</a> airfield on 20 May 1944, showing the layout of the <a href="/wiki/Runway" title="Runway">runways</a> that allow aircraft to take off and land into the wind</div></div></div>
<p>There are many different forms of sailing ships, but they all have certain basic things in common. Except for <a href="/wiki/Rotor_ship" title="Rotor ship">rotor ships</a> using the <a href="/wiki/Magnus_effect" title="Magnus effect">Magnus effect</a>, every sailing ship has a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hull_(ship)" title="Hull (ship)">hull</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rigging" title="Rigging">rigging</a> and at least one <a href="/wiki/Mast_(sailing)" title="Mast (sailing)">mast</a> to hold up the <a href="/wiki/Sail" title="Sail">sails</a> that use the wind to power the ship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> Ocean journeys by sailing ship can take many months,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> and a common hazard is becoming becalmed because of lack of wind,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> or being blown off course by severe <a href="/wiki/Storm" title="Storm">storms</a> or winds that do not allow progress in the desired direction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> A severe storm could lead to <a href="/wiki/Shipwreck" title="Shipwreck">shipwreck</a>, and the loss of all hands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Sailing ships can only carry a certain quantity of supplies in their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hold_(ship)" title="Hold (ship)">hold</a>, so they have to plan long <a href="/wiki/Maritime_history" title="Maritime history">voyages</a> carefully to include appropriate <a href="/wiki/Provisioning_of_USS_Constitution" title="Provisioning of USS Constitution">provisions</a>, including fresh water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>For <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aerodynamic" title="Aerodynamic">aerodynamic</a> aircraft which operate relative to the air, winds affect groundspeed,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> and in the case of lighter-than-air vehicles, wind may play a significant or solitary role in their movement and <a href="/wiki/Ground_track" title="Ground track">ground track</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Velocity" title="Velocity">velocity</a> of surface wind is generally the primary factor governing the direction of flight operations at an airport, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Airfield" title="Airfield">airfield</a> runways are aligned to account for the common wind direction(s) of the local area. While taking off with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tailwind" title="Tailwind">tailwind</a> may be necessary under certain circumstances, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Headwind" title="Headwind">headwind</a> is generally desirable. A tailwind increases takeoff distance required and decreases the climb gradient.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Power_source">Power source</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Power source">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Windenergy.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1043" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Windenergy.jpg/220px-Windenergy.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Windenergy.jpg/330px-Windenergy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Windenergy.jpg/440px-Windenergy.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Windenergy.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This <a href="/wiki/Wind_turbine" title="Wind turbine">wind turbine</a> generates electricity from wind power.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Wind_power" title="Wind power">Wind power</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wind_atlas" title="Wind atlas">Wind atlas</a></div>
<p>The ancient <a href="/wiki/Sinhalese_people" title="Sinhalese people">Sinhalese</a> of <a href="/wiki/Anuradhapura" title="Anuradhapura">Anuradhapura</a> and in other cities around <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a> used the monsoon winds to power furnaces as early as 300 <a href="/wiki/Common_Era" title="Common Era">BCE</a>. The furnaces were constructed on the path of the monsoon winds to bring the temperatures inside up to 1,200 °C (2,190 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> A rudimentary <a href="/wiki/Windmill" title="Windmill">windmill</a> was used to power an <a href="/wiki/Organ_(music)" title="Organ (music)">organ</a> in the first century CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> Windmills were later built in <a href="/wiki/Sistan" title="Sistan">Sistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>, from the 7th century CE. These were vertical-axle windmills,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> with <a href="/wiki/Windmill_sail" title="Windmill sail">sails</a> covered in <a href="/wiki/Reed_mat_(craft)" title="Reed mat (craft)">reed matting</a> or cloth material. These windmills were used to grind corn and draw up water, and were used in the <a href="/wiki/Gristmill" title="Gristmill">gristmilling</a> and sugarcane industries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> Horizontal-axle windmills were later used extensively in Northwestern Europe to grind flour beginning in the 1180s, and many Dutch windmills still exist.
</p><p>Wind power is now one of the main sources of <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable energy</a>, and its use is growing rapidly, driven by innovation and falling prices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> Most of the installed capacity in wind power is <a href="/wiki/Wind_farm" title="Wind farm">onshore</a>, but <a href="/wiki/Offshore_wind_power" title="Offshore wind power">offshore wind power</a> offers a large potential as wind speeds are typically higher and more constant away from the coast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> Wind energy the <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">kinetic energy</a> of the air, is proportional to the third power of wind velocity. <a href="/wiki/Betz%27s_law" title="Betz's law">Betz's law</a> described the theoretical upper limit of what fraction of this energy wind turbines can extract, which is about 59%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recreation">Recreation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Recreation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lilienthal_in_flight.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="365" data-file-width="504" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Lilienthal_in_flight.jpg/220px-Lilienthal_in_flight.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Lilienthal_in_flight.jpg/330px-Lilienthal_in_flight.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Lilienthal_in_flight.jpg/440px-Lilienthal_in_flight.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lilienthal_in_flight.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Otto_Lilienthal" title="Otto Lilienthal">Otto Lilienthal</a> in flight</div></div></div>
<p>Wind figures prominently in several popular sports, including recreational <a href="/wiki/Hang_gliding" title="Hang gliding">hang gliding</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hot_air_ballooning" title="Hot air ballooning">hot air ballooning</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kite" title="Kite">kite</a> flying, <a href="/wiki/Snowkiting" title="Snowkiting">snowkiting</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kite_landboarding" title="Kite landboarding">kite landboarding</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kite_surfing" title="Kite surfing">kite surfing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paragliding" title="Paragliding">paragliding</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sailing" title="Sailing">sailing</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Windsurfing" title="Windsurfing">windsurfing</a>. In gliding, wind gradients just above the surface affect the takeoff and landing phases of flight of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glider_aircraft" title="Glider aircraft">glider</a>. Wind gradient can have a noticeable effect on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ground_launch" title="Ground launch">ground launches</a>, also known as winch launches or wire launches. If the wind gradient is significant or sudden, or both, and the pilot maintains the same pitch attitude, the indicated airspeed will increase, possibly exceeding the maximum ground launch tow speed. The pilot must adjust the airspeed to deal with the effect of the gradient.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> When landing, wind shear is also a hazard, particularly when the winds are strong. As the glider descends through the wind gradient on final approach to landing, airspeed decreases while sink rate increases, and there is insufficient time to accelerate prior to ground contact. The pilot must anticipate the wind gradient and use a higher approach speed to compensate for it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Piggott_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Piggott-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_the_natural_world">In the natural world</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: In the natural world">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Aeolian_processes" title="Aeolian processes">Aeolian processes</a></div>
<p>In arid climates, the main source of erosion is wind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Erosion_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Erosion-100">[100]</a></sup> The general wind circulation moves small particulates such as dust across wide oceans thousands of kilometers downwind of their point of origin,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gobi_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gobi-101">[101]</a></sup> which is known as deflation. Westerly winds in the mid-latitudes of the planet drive the movement of ocean currents from west to east across the world's oceans. Wind has a very important role in aiding plants and other immobile organisms in dispersal of seeds, spores, pollen, etc. Although wind is not the primary form of seed dispersal in plants, it provides dispersal for a large percentage of the biomass of land plants.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Erosion">Erosion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Erosion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Im_Salar_de_Uyuni.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1544" data-file-width="2080" decoding="async" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Im_Salar_de_Uyuni.jpg/220px-Im_Salar_de_Uyuni.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Im_Salar_de_Uyuni.jpg/330px-Im_Salar_de_Uyuni.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Im_Salar_de_Uyuni.jpg/440px-Im_Salar_de_Uyuni.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Im_Salar_de_Uyuni.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A rock formation in the <a href="/wiki/Altiplano" title="Altiplano">Altiplano</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bolivia" title="Bolivia">Bolivia</a>, sculpted by wind erosion</div></div></div>
<p>Erosion can be the result of material movement by the wind. There are two main effects. First, wind causes small particles to be lifted and therefore moved to another region. This is called deflation. Second, these suspended particles may impact on solid objects causing erosion by abrasion (ecological succession). Wind erosion generally occurs in areas with little or no vegetation, often in areas where there is insufficient rainfall to support vegetation. An example is the formation of sand <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dunes" title="Dunes">dunes</a>, on a beach or in a desert.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> Loess is a homogeneous, typically nonstratified, porous, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Friable" title="Friable">friable</a>, slightly coherent, often calcareous, fine-grained, <a href="/wiki/Silt" title="Silt">silty</a>, pale yellow or buff, windblown (Aeolian) <a href="/wiki/Sediment" title="Sediment">sediment</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> It generally occurs as a widespread blanket deposit that covers areas of hundreds of square kilometers and tens of meters thick. Loess often stands in either steep or vertical faces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> Loess tends to develop into highly rich soils. Under appropriate climatic conditions, areas with loess are among the most agriculturally productive in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> Loess deposits are geologically unstable by nature, and will erode very readily. Therefore, <a href="/wiki/Windbreak" title="Windbreak">windbreaks</a> (such as big trees and bushes) are often planted by farmers to reduce the wind erosion of loess.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Erosion_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-Erosion-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Desert_dust_migration">Desert dust migration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Desert dust migration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>During mid-summer (July in the northern hemisphere), the westward-moving trade winds south of the northward-moving subtropical ridge expand northwestward from the Caribbean into southeastern North America. When dust from the <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">Sahara</a> moving around the southern periphery of the ridge within the belt of trade winds moves over land, rainfall is suppressed and the sky changes from a blue to a white appearance, which leads to an increase in red sunsets. Its presence negatively impacts <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Air_quality" title="Air quality">air quality</a> by adding to the count of airborne particulates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-106">[106]</a></sup> Over 50% of the African dust that reaches the United States affects Florida.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> Since 1970, dust outbreaks have worsened because of periods of drought in Africa. There is a large variability in the dust transport to the Caribbean and Florida from year to year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> Dust events have been linked to a decline in the health of <a href="/wiki/Coral_reef" title="Coral reef">coral reefs</a> across the Caribbean and Florida, primarily since the 1970s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> Similar dust plumes originate in the <a href="/wiki/Gobi_Desert" title="Gobi Desert">Gobi Desert</a>, which combined with pollutants, spread large distances downwind, or eastward, into North America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gobi_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gobi-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are local names for winds associated with sand and dust storms.
