The Calima carries dust on southeast winds into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canary_islands" title="Canary islands">Canary islands</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Harmattan" title="Harmattan">Harmattan</a> carries dust during the winter into the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Guinea" title="Gulf of Guinea">Gulf of Guinea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Sirocco" title="Sirocco">Sirocco</a> brings dust from north Africa into southern Europe because of the movement of extratropical cyclones through the Mediterranean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> Spring storm systems moving across the eastern Mediterranean Sea cause dust to carry across <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabian_peninsula" title="Arabian peninsula">Arabian peninsula</a>, which are locally known as <a href="/wiki/Khamsin" title="Khamsin">Khamsin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Shamal_(wind)" title="Shamal (wind)">Shamal</a> is caused by cold fronts lifting dust into the atmosphere for days at a time across the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a> states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Effect_on_plants">Effect on plants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Effect on plants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Salsola_tragus_tumbleweed.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="450" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Salsola_tragus_tumbleweed.jpg/220px-Salsola_tragus_tumbleweed.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Salsola_tragus_tumbleweed.jpg/330px-Salsola_tragus_tumbleweed.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Salsola_tragus_tumbleweed.jpg/440px-Salsola_tragus_tumbleweed.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Salsola_tragus_tumbleweed.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tumbleweed blown against a fence</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coarse_woody_debris_6407.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="4272" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Coarse_woody_debris_6407.JPG/220px-Coarse_woody_debris_6407.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Coarse_woody_debris_6407.JPG/330px-Coarse_woody_debris_6407.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Coarse_woody_debris_6407.JPG/440px-Coarse_woody_debris_6407.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Coarse_woody_debris_6407.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Montane_ecology" title="Montane ecology">montane forest</a> of <a href="/wiki/Olympic_National_Park" title="Olympic National Park">Olympic National Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Windthrow" title="Windthrow">windthrow</a> opens the <a href="/wiki/Canopy_(biology)" title="Canopy (biology)">canopy</a> and increases light intensity on the <a href="/wiki/Understory" title="Understory">understory</a>.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Seed_dispersal" title="Seed dispersal">Seed dispersal</a></div>
<p>Wind dispersal of seeds, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anemochory" title="Anemochory">anemochory</a>, is one of the more primitive means of dispersal. Wind dispersal can take on one of two primary forms: seeds can float on the breeze or alternatively, they can flutter to the ground.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> The classic examples of these dispersal mechanisms include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dandelion" title="Dandelion">dandelions</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Taraxacum" title="Taraxacum">Taraxacum</a></i> spp., <a href="/wiki/Asteraceae" title="Asteraceae">Asteraceae</a>), which have a feathery <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pappus_(flower_structure)" title="Pappus (flower structure)">pappus</a> attached to their seeds and can be dispersed long distances, and <a href="/wiki/Maple" title="Maple">maples</a> (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acer_(genus)" title="Acer (genus)">Acer (genus)</a></i> spp., <a href="/wiki/Sapindaceae" title="Sapindaceae">Sapindaceae</a>), which have winged seeds and flutter to the ground. An important constraint on wind dispersal is the need for abundant seed production to maximize the likelihood of a seed landing in a site suitable for <a href="/wiki/Germination" title="Germination">germination</a>. There are also strong evolutionary constraints on this dispersal mechanism. For instance, species in the Asteraceae on islands tended to have reduced dispersal capabilities (i.e., larger seed mass and smaller pappus) relative to the same species on the mainland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> Reliance upon wind dispersal is common among many <a href="/wiki/Weed" title="Weed">weedy</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ruderal" title="Ruderal">ruderal</a> species. Unusual mechanisms of wind dispersal include <a href="/wiki/Tumbleweed" title="Tumbleweed">tumbleweeds</a>. A related process to anemochory is <a href="/wiki/Anemophily" title="Anemophily">anemophily</a>, which is the process where pollen is distributed by wind. Large families of plants are pollinated in this manner, which is favored when individuals of the dominant plant species are spaced closely together.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>Wind also limits tree growth. On coasts and isolated mountains, the tree line is often much lower than in corresponding altitudes inland and in larger, more complex mountain systems, because strong winds reduce tree growth. High winds scour away thin <a href="/wiki/Soil" title="Soil">soils</a> through erosion,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> as well as damage limbs and twigs. When high winds knock down or uproot trees, the process is known as <a href="/wiki/Windthrow" title="Windthrow">windthrow</a>. This is most likely on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Windward" title="Windward">windward</a> slopes of mountains, with severe cases generally occurring to <a href="/wiki/Tree_stand" title="Tree stand">tree stands</a> that are 75 years or older.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> Plant varieties near the coast, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sitka_spruce" title="Sitka spruce">Sitka spruce</a> and <a href="/wiki/Coccoloba_uvifera" title="Coccoloba uvifera">sea grape</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> are <a href="/wiki/Pruning" title="Pruning">pruned</a> back by wind and salt spray near the coastline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup>
</p><p>Wind can also cause plants damage through <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Sand_abrasion&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Sand abrasion (page does not exist)">sand abrasion</a>. Strong winds will pick up loose sand and <a href="/wiki/Topsoil" title="Topsoil">topsoil</a> and hurl it through the air at speeds ranging from 25 miles per hour (40 km/h) to 40 miles per hour (64 km/h). Such windblown sand causes extensive damage to plant seedlings because it ruptures plant cells, making them vulnerable to evaporation and drought. Using a mechanical sandblaster in a laboratory setting, scientists affiliated with the <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_Research_Service" title="Agricultural Research Service">Agricultural Research Service</a> studied the effects of windblown sand abrasion on cotton seedlings. The study showed that the seedlings responded to the damage created by the windblown sand abrasion by shifting energy from stem and root growth to the growth and repair of the damaged stems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> After a period of four weeks, the growth of the seedling once again became uniform throughout the plant, as it was before the windblown sand abrasion occurred.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup>
</p><p>Besides plant gametes (seeds) wind also helps plants' enemies: <a href="/wiki/Spore" title="Spore">Spores</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Propagule" title="Propagule">propagules</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plant_pathogen" title="Plant pathogen">plant pathogens</a> are even lighter and able to travel long distances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wilson-Talbot-2009_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wilson-Talbot-2009-124">[124]</a></sup> A few plant diseases are known to have been known to travel over <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marginal_sea" title="Marginal sea">marginal seas</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morin-2020_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Morin-2020-125">[125]</a></sup> and even entire oceans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rust-SAF-Aust_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-rust-SAF-Aust-126">[126]</a></sup> Humans are unable to prevent or even slow down wind dispersal of plant pathogens, requiring prediction and amelioration instead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McDonald-Linde-2002_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-McDonald-Linde-2002-127">[127]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Effect_on_animals">Effect on animals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Effect on animals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Cattle" title="Cattle">Cattle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sheep" title="Sheep">sheep</a> are prone to <a href="/wiki/Wind_chill" title="Wind chill">wind chill</a> caused by a combination of wind and cold temperatures, when winds exceed 40 kilometers per hour (25 mph), rendering their hair and wool coverings ineffective.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> Although <a href="/wiki/Penguin" title="Penguin">penguins</a> use both a layer of <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fat</a> and <a href="/wiki/Feather" title="Feather">feathers</a> to help guard against coldness in both water and air, their <a href="/wiki/Flipper_(anatomy)" title="Flipper (anatomy)">flippers</a> and feet are less immune to the cold. In the coldest climates such as <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emperor_penguin" title="Emperor penguin">emperor penguins</a> use <a href="/wiki/Kleptothermy" title="Kleptothermy">huddling</a> behavior to survive the wind and cold, continuously alternating the members on the outside of the assembled group, which reduces heat loss by 50%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> Flying <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a>, a subset of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arthropods" title="Arthropods">arthropods</a>, are swept along by the prevailing winds,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> while birds follow their own course taking advantage of wind conditions, in order to either fly or glide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> As such, fine line patterns within <a href="/wiki/Weather_radar" title="Weather radar">weather radar</a> imagery, associated with converging winds, are dominated by insect returns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> Bird migration, which tends to occur overnight within the lowest 7,000 feet (2,100 m) of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Earth's atmosphere">Earth's atmosphere</a>, contaminates wind profiles gathered by weather radar, particularly the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WSR-88D" title="WSR-88D">WSR-88D</a>, by increasing the environmental wind returns by 15 knots (28 km/h) to 30 knots (56 km/h).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Pika" title="Pika">Pikas</a> use a wall of pebbles to store dry plants and grasses for the winter in order to protect the food from being blown away.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cockroach" title="Cockroach">Cockroaches</a> use slight winds that precede the attacks of potential <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Predator" title="Predator">predators</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Toad" title="Toad">toads</a>, to survive their encounters. Their <a href="/wiki/Cercus" title="Cercus">cerci</a> are very sensitive to the wind, and help them survive half of their attacks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Elk" title="Elk">Elk</a> have a keen sense of smell that can detect potential upwind predators at a distance of 0.5 miles (800 m).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> Increases in wind above 15 kilometers per hour (9.3 mph) signals <a href="/wiki/Glaucous_gull" title="Glaucous gull">glaucous gulls</a> to increase their foraging and aerial attacks on thick-billed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Murres" title="Murres">murres</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Related_damage">Related damage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Related damage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Severe_weather" title="Severe weather">Severe weather</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Destruction_following_hurricane_andrew.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1832" decoding="async" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Destruction_following_hurricane_andrew.jpg/220px-Destruction_following_hurricane_andrew.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Destruction_following_hurricane_andrew.jpg/330px-Destruction_following_hurricane_andrew.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Destruction_following_hurricane_andrew.jpg/440px-Destruction_following_hurricane_andrew.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Destruction_following_hurricane_andrew.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Damage from <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Andrew" title="Hurricane Andrew">Hurricane Andrew</a></div></div></div>
<p>High winds are known to cause damage, depending upon the magnitude of their velocity and pressure differential. Wind pressures are positive on the windward side of a structure and negative on the leeward side. Infrequent wind gusts can cause poorly designed <a href="/wiki/Suspension_bridge" title="Suspension bridge">suspension bridges</a> to sway. When wind gusts are at a similar frequency to the swaying of the bridge, the bridge can be destroyed more easily, such as what occurred with the <a href="/wiki/Tacoma_Narrows_Bridge_(1940)" title="Tacoma Narrows Bridge (1940)">Tacoma Narrows Bridge</a> in 1940.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> Wind speeds as low as 23 knots (43 km/h) can lead to power outages due to tree branches disrupting the flow of energy through power lines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> While no species of tree is guaranteed to stand up to hurricane-force winds, those with shallow roots are more prone to uproot, and brittle trees such as <a href="/wiki/Eucalyptus" title="Eucalyptus">eucalyptus</a>, sea <a href="/wiki/Hibiscus" title="Hibiscus">hibiscus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Avocado" title="Avocado">avocado</a> are more prone to damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> Hurricane-force winds cause substantial damage to mobile homes, and begin to structurally damage homes with foundations. Winds of this strength due to downsloped winds off terrain have been known to shatter windows and sandblast paint from cars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-boulder_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-boulder-57">[57]</a></sup> Once winds exceed 135 knots (250 km/h), homes completely collapse, and significant damage is done to larger buildings. Total destruction to artificial structures occurs when winds reach 175 knots (324 km/h). The <a href="/wiki/Saffir%E2%80%93Simpson_scale" title="Saffir–Simpson scale">Saffir–Simpson scale</a> and <a href="/wiki/Enhanced_Fujita_scale" title="Enhanced Fujita scale">Enhanced Fujita scale</a> were designed to help estimate wind speed from the damage caused by high winds related to tropical cyclones and <a href="/wiki/Tornado" title="Tornado">tornadoes</a>, and vice versa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NHC_SSHS_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-NHC_SSHS-141">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EF_SPC_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-EF_SPC-142">[142]</a></sup>
</p><p>Australia's <a href="/wiki/Barrow_Island_(Western_Australia)" title="Barrow Island (Western Australia)">Barrow Island</a> holds the record for the strongest wind gust, reaching 408 km/h (253 mph) during tropical cyclone Olivia on 10 April 1996, surpassing the previous record of 372 km/h (231 mph) set on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mount_Washington_(New_Hampshire)" title="Mount Washington (New Hampshire)">Mount Washington (New Hampshire)</a> on the afternoon of 12 April 1934.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup>
</p><p>Wildfire intensity increases during daytime hours. For example, burn rates of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Smoldering" title="Smoldering">smoldering</a> logs are up to five times greater during the day because of lower humidity, increased temperatures, and increased wind speeds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> Sunlight warms the ground during the day and causes air currents to travel uphill, and downhill during the night as the land cools. Wildfires are fanned by these winds and often follow the air currents over hills and through valleys.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> United States wildfire operations revolve around a 24-hour <i>fire day</i> that begins at 10:00 a.m. because of the predictable increase in intensity resulting from the daytime warmth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_outer_space">In outer space</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: In outer space">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Stellar_wind" title="Stellar wind">Stellar wind</a></div>
<p>The solar wind is quite different from a terrestrial wind, in that its origin is the Sun, and it is composed of charged particles that have escaped the Sun's atmosphere. Similar to the solar wind, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planetary_wind" title="Planetary wind">planetary wind</a> is composed of light gases that escape planetary atmospheres. Over long periods of time, the planetary wind can radically change the composition of planetary atmospheres.
</p><p>The fastest wind ever recorded came from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Accretion_disc" title="Accretion disc">accretion disc</a> of the <a href="/wiki/IGR_J17091-3624" title="IGR J17091-3624">IGR J17091-3624</a> black hole. Its speed is 20,000,000 miles per hour (32,000,000 km/h), which is 3% of the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Planetary_wind">Planetary wind</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Planetary wind">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_escape" title="Atmospheric escape">Atmospheric escape</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Venuspioneeruv.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2158" data-file-width="2008" decoding="async" height="236" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Venuspioneeruv.jpg/220px-Venuspioneeruv.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Venuspioneeruv.jpg/330px-Venuspioneeruv.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Venuspioneeruv.jpg/440px-Venuspioneeruv.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Venuspioneeruv.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A possible future for Earth due to the planetary wind: Venus</div></div></div>
<p>The hydrodynamic wind within the upper portion of a planet's atmosphere allows light chemical elements such as <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> to move up to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exobase" title="Exobase">exobase</a>, the lower limit of the <a href="/wiki/Exosphere" title="Exosphere">exosphere</a>, where the gases can then reach <a href="/wiki/Escape_velocity" title="Escape velocity">escape velocity</a>, entering outer space without impacting other particles of gas. This type of gas loss from a planet into space is known as planetary wind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> Such a process over <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geologic_time" title="Geologic time">geologic time</a> causes water-rich planets such as the Earth to evolve into planets like <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> Additionally, planets with hotter lower atmospheres could accelerate the loss rate of hydrogen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dvorak_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dvorak-150">[150]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Solar_wind">Solar wind</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Solar wind">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">Solar wind</a></div>
<p>Rather than air, the solar wind is a <a href="/wiki/Electric_current" title="Electric current">stream of charged particles</a>—a <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">plasma</a>—ejected from the <a href="/wiki/Stellar_atmosphere" title="Stellar atmosphere">upper atmosphere</a> of the sun at a rate of 400 kilometers per second (890,000 mph). It consists mostly of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrons" title="Electrons">electrons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">protons</a> with energies of about 1 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electron_volt" title="Electron volt">keV</a>. The stream of particles varies in temperature and speed with the passage of time. These particles are able to escape the Sun's <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a>, in part because of the high <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">temperature</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_corona" title="Solar corona">corona</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> but also because of high kinetic energy that particles gain through a process that is not well understood. The solar wind creates the <a href="/wiki/Heliosphere" title="Heliosphere">Heliosphere</a>, a vast bubble in the <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">interstellar medium</a> surrounding the Solar System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> Planets require large magnetic fields in order to reduce the ionization of their upper atmosphere by the solar wind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dvorak_150-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dvorak-150">[150]</a></sup> Other phenomena caused by the solar wind include <a href="/wiki/Geomagnetic_storm" title="Geomagnetic storm">geomagnetic storms</a> that can knock out power grids on Earth,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aurora_(phenomenon)" title="Aurora (phenomenon)">aurorae</a> such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aurora_(astronomy)" title="Aurora (astronomy)">Northern Lights</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> and the plasma tails of <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comets</a> that always point away from the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="On_other_planets">On other planets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: On other planets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Strong 300 kilometers per hour (190 mph) winds at Venus's cloud tops circle the planet every four to five earth days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> When the poles of <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> are exposed to sunlight after their winter, the frozen CO<sub>2</sub> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sublimation_(physics)" title="Sublimation (physics)">sublimates</a>, creating significant winds that sweep off the poles as fast as 400 kilometers per hour (250 mph), which subsequently transports large amounts of dust and water vapor over its <a href="/wiki/Landscape" title="Landscape">landscape</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clouds_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-clouds-157">[157]</a></sup> Other Martian winds have resulted in <a href="/wiki/Cleaning_event" title="Cleaning event">cleaning events</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spirit_rover#Dust_devils" title="Spirit rover">dust devils</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> On <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>, wind speeds of 100 meters per second (220 mph) are common in zonal jet streams.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> Saturn's winds are among the Solar System's fastest. <a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini–Huygens">Cassini–Huygens</a> data indicated peak easterly winds of 375 meters per second (840 mph).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> On <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a>, northern hemisphere wind speeds reach as high as 240 meters per second (540 mph) near 50 degrees north latitude.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sromovsky_&amp;_Fry_2005_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sromovsky_&amp;_Fry_2005-162">[162]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hammel_de_Pater_et_al._Uranus_in_2003,_2005_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hammel_de_Pater_et_al._Uranus_in_2003,_2005-163">[163]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hammel_Rages_et_al._2001_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hammel_Rages_et_al._2001-164">[164]</a></sup> At the cloud tops of <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>, prevailing winds range in speed from 400 meters per second (890 mph) along the equator to 250 meters per second (560 mph) at the poles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elkins-tanton_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-elkins-tanton-165">[165]</a></sup> At 70° S latitude on Neptune, a high-speed jet stream travels at a speed of 300 meters per second (670 mph).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lunine_1993_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lunine_1993-166">[166]</a></sup> The fastest wind on any known planet is on <a href="/wiki/HD_80606_b" title="HD 80606 b">HD 80606 b</a> located 190 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Light_year" title="Light year">light years</a> away, where it blows at more than 11,000 mph or 5 km/s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup>
</p>
